		<username>ackstasis</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>"Listen, Little Boy, in this business there's only one law you gotta follow to keep out of trouble: Do it first, do it yourself, and keep on doing it."</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>'Scarface,' produced by Howard Hughes and Howards Hawks, and directed by the latter, was completed in 1930, but was deemed too controversial by the censers, who declared that the film glorified the gangster life and portrayed too much violence. It was demanded that several scenes must be edited, an alternate ending added and the subtitle 'The Shame of the Nation' tacked onto the end of the title. However, Hawks disowned this new version of his film, and it was created without him. Fortunately, the version that most people see nowadays is Hawks' original vision, complete with excessive violence (for its time) and the bitter, bloody downfall of Tony Camonte (Paul Muni) at the hands of police.The film, without a doubt, was an attempt to make a political statement. It opens with title cards declaring, "This picture is an indictment of gang rule in America and of the callous indifference of the government to this constantly increasing menace to our safety and our liberty. Every incident in this picture is the reproduction of an actual occurrence, and the purpose of this picture is to demand of the government: 'What are you going to do about it?' The government is your government. What are YOU going to do about it?""This is followed by a scene in the middle of the film, in which the newspaper publisher proclaims ? somewhat uncharacteristically- that the blame for these gang wars should not be placed on the police, but firmly upon the government, and the civilians who do not speak out against them. After all this is out of the way, however, we are allowed back into the fantastic story of Tony Camonte's rise and downfall, and we can forgive the filmmakers' now-dated political motives.Tony Camonte, played perfectly by Paul Muni, is a smug, insatiably-violent gangster who is dismissive of authority, both in the police force and in his own criminal organization. He operates under the orders of Johnny Lovo (Osgood Perkins), whom he neither respects nor really listens to, and his loyal friend and bodyguard is Guino Rinaldo (George Raft), whom Camonte knows would never betray him. Feared by his own mother for years, Camonte is also fiercely protective of his eighteen-year-old sister, Francesca (Ann Dvorak). Despite her insistence that she is now old enough to take care of herself, Camonte reacts violently whenever he spies a man giving his sister the least bit of attention, and he offers Francesca money so she can enjoy herself through other means. Camonte's influence, however, inevitably turns Francesca into what he most feared: himself.At the end of the film, as power, greed and arrogance have torn Camonte's world apart, he stumbles weakly down the staircase to his home, and a bullet hurls the weapon from his grasp. A pathetic mess, the once-powerful Tony Camonte begs for mercy from the police, fruitlessly trying to justify his actions. The police offer him an opportunity to surrender, but Camonte makes a final break for freedom, and police weapons blaze him down like a piece of vermin. Peppered with bullet wounds, Camonte's deceased body hits the hard ground, the camera slowly rising to reveal the sign near which he died, ironically reading: "The World Is Yours."</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2001-04-01</date>
		<username>Glenn Andreiev (gandreiev@aol.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Hold On! A Wild Ride From 1932!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Contains SpoilerIt's a format we've seen in gangster films a zillion times. Street hood climbs the criminal corporate ladder. It seems fun at first. Lots of wild action, being able to act tough, plenty of dames. But then, it gets ugly. SCARFACE was one the first films to use this format. It leaves contemporary gangster classics (LITTLE CAESER and PUBLIC ENEMY) in the dust! We are riveted to the screen by Paul Muni's wacked title role performance. Usually in a Howard Hawks film, the characters are likeable, people we want to be around, but not Muni's Tony Camonte. That's what makes SCARFACE so amazingly fascinating! We can safely observe Camonte's violent and ugly world without participating! The centerpiece of the film is the gang war that explodes over control of a cities booze racket. To signify a hit is to take place, or is in the process of taking place, an X is prominently in the scene. It's either a crossbeam, the Roman Numeral "Ten" on a door, looking down at an intersection street sign. The most brillaint use of this is on a bowling scorecard. We see Boris Karloff get his during a bowling game. He is the final target amongst Camonte's rivals. The camera leaves him as bullets tear into him. He is about to bowl a strike. The camera follows the ball as it hits the pins, except for one, that takes forever to spin and topple. This is what film is all about- the visual presentation.Easily one of the ten best pre-World War II films.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>matt-128</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Excellent</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Scarface is one of the first great mob movies. Paul Muni plays Tony Camonte in a performance that never feels dated or boring; it bursts with sick energy. He makes these strange gestures, which he probably learned on the stage, to entice the other characters to play along with him; like he'll start making popping sounds, as he plucks his hand off his temple. He also shifts his emotions very abruptly, and you cannot really predict when he'll lose his temper, or when he'll act charming and witty. There is a scene when he directly implies to Poppy (Karen Morley) that he wants to have sex, and you can really see his great comic timing. His performance is ten times better than any other Cagney-mobster performance; so why am I the only one that knows it. Howard Hawkes directs with great vision and excellence. He made this into a very violent film for its time, but he also made some great visuals on the screen. Such as his use of the "X" every time someone is about to die or after they die. And who could forget that scene where the machine gun blasts the dates away on the calender. I think he was still learning how to make a movie, because some of the scenes just don't seem like they should be in this one. It never grows to the stature of "The Godfather," or "Goodfellas," but I think it's right behind them. And those films probably would have never been made if it wasn't for "Scarface."</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>My shutters, they don't work!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>(There are Spoilers) Unoubtaly the best of the depression era gangster movies that came out of Hollywood in the 1930's "Scarface" was so chilling and effective that it had it's director Howard Hawks, who owed the film,lock up all the prints and take the movie out of circulation back in 1932 the year it was released. It wasn't until two years after Hawks death in December 1977 that "Scarface" was ever seen by the public again. Spinning off a remake in 1983 staring Al Pacino the original that's well over 70 years old still eclipses the later, made in color and with far more graphic sex and violence, by a country mile.Vicious and brazen Tony Camonte, Paul Muni, has big plans for himself in getting to the top of the heap in the criminal underworld in 1920's Chicago. Being "Big Louie" Costello's (Harry J. Vejar), the mob boss of Chicago's first district, bodyguard Tony blows him away after the party thrown for him is over and everyone left. Getting on board with the South Side's top mobster and bootlegger Johnny Lovo, Osgood Perkins, Tony's brutal and uncompromising strong-arming of the local illegal bars and speakeasy's have Lovo gain complete control of the South Side of Chicago's lucrative booze business. But Tony has bigger plans not for his boss Johnny Lovo but for himself. Having the Irish boss O'Hara of the powerful North Side Mob knocked off, against Johnny Lovo's, orders turns Chicago into a full-fledged war zone. With hundreds of automatic sub-machine guns imported into the city from out of state and used in dozens of deadly shoot-outs. This results in scores of innocent persons, as well as mobsters, getting killed and wounded. Tony's unrelenting ruthlessness has the North Side Mob on the run and just about finishes them off in a bloody wear-house massacre, on Valentine Day, with seven members of the gang machine gunned to death. Gaffney, Boris Karloff, O'Hara's North Side Mob replacement is now a marked man with him just escaping Tony's Valentine Day killing. With his gang now no longer a factor in the crime scene in Chicago has Gaffney just waiting for the inevitable to happen. The end comes in a bowling alley where it turns out Gaffney plays his last game of ten pins. It's when Tony finally reaches the top that he loses his edge in keeping his crime organization together and where his paranoia takes over. Feeling that his boss, what a laugh, Johnny Lovo is trying to knock him off after an unsuccessful assassination attempt on him Tony pay's Johnny a visit with his trusted friend and fellow hoodlum Gino "Flip" Rinaldo, George Raft,and blows him away. Meanwhile the local police working together with the Federal Government gather all the information that they could to get enough evidence to put Tony away for a long time. Ironically it's Tony himself that gives them all the rope that they need to hang him.Finding out that his chorusing 18 year-old sister Cesca (Ann Dvorak), whom he tried to keep to himself and away from any suitors, moved out of her parents house Tony finds where she lives in the city and pays Cesca a surprise visit. Seeing his life-long friend Gino answer the door and Cesca in a nightgown flips Tony out and before he can think blasts Gino away. It turned out that both Gino and Cesca were married while Tony was away in Florida and wanted to surprise him with the "good" new! But the shoot first and ask questions later Tony Camonte didn't bother to wait around to hear it. Broken and abandoned Tony is now left all alone at his reinforced bunker-like apartment with no one but Cesca by his side as he waits for the police to assault and storm it. The only hope he has now is to go down fighting and not be taken alive. They don't make them, gangster films, any better then "Scarface" and it's amazing to watch the movie now and see how ahead of it's time it really was. In fact it's time, and modern day gangster/crime films, still hasn't caught up with it. P.S Unlike in action/gangster movies made today the shooting scenes in "Scarface" had real bullets used in it's the films action sequences! Hollywood studios didn't have gunfire special effects back then in 1932 like they do now and had to use,live bullets, whatever was available to them. It's said that actor Paul Muni was so shocked by the negative reaction he got from the pubic, in his role as gangster Tony Camonte in the movie, that for almost 15 years he refused to take another gangster role. Even though Muni-in a way- finally gave in and played one in the life-after-death comedy/drama "Angel on my Shoulder" in 1946.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>It's a typewriter. I'm gonna write my name all over this town with it, in big letters!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Upon started this film I was greeted by a message I really just had no memory of from my first viewing. A very striking message that gives all the violence and brutality in this film (especially for the 30s) a powerful impact. This message declares "This picture is an indictment of gang rule in America and of the callous indifference of the government to this constantly increasing menace to our safety and our liberty. Every incident in this picture is the reproduction of an actual occurrence, and the purpose of this picture is to demand of the government: 'What are you going to do about it?' The government is your government. What are YOU going to do about it?" This gives the film a significant historical context. The people of this era lived with the horrific violence portrayed in the film. It wasn't just a film for the viewers of the time. These were the actions that made this era's gangsters thrive. I've always known Scarface was based on the crimes committed by Al Capone, but being given a reminder in the film's opening credits makes its portrayal chilling. The destruction and collateral damage Tony was able to wreak upon Chicago with his Thompson? The children that ended up getting "bellies full of lead"? All included in the film to illuminate a truth. This made the film very hard to stomach, but the passion it gave to its writers, directors, and outstanding lead Paul Muni just make it all the more excellent. If you love gangster movies or American history like I do, this is an absolute must see.</fullReview>

		<date>2014-08-23</date>
		<username>bayardhiler</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The Original Gangster Epic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Long before Brian De Palma directed his '83 masterpiece, there was an original Tony looking to grab the American dream, whatever the cost. Made in 1932, this version of "Scarface" made quite a stir in its day, due to its boldness and refusal to sugarcoat the brutal reality of the gang warfare of the times. Like its '83 counterpart, the movie follows the rise and fall of a hood named Tony. Instead of the seedy, decadent Miami of the 80's, though, this Tony's era is 1920's Chicago, where prohibition is in full swing and the streets are ever flowing with blood. It's in this setting that we first meet Antonio "Tony" Camonte (Paul Muni in a tour de force performance) aka Scarface. Tough, ambitious, ruthless, and slightly crazed (Especially when it comes to the love for his sister), Tony is determined to reach the top of the Chicago underworld by murdering his boss, "Big" Louis Costillo and subsequently going behind the back of his new one, Johnny Lovo, for the sake of greater profits and to have Johnny's girl for himself. Soon though, Tony's actions spark a gang war with the North Side gang and from there it's nothing short of a barrage of shootings, bombings, and murders. Eventually, Tony has everything he could possibly want and more. But just like Al Pacino, his madness and lust threaten to bring everything he's built down the drain.Filmed in eerie black and white, the film still holds up well after all these years. Part of this would be due to the brilliant direction of Howard Hawks, who takes a simple gangster picture and manages to make it into something more with his creative camera shots and ominous cues when someone is about to die (watch for the Xs and don't be near Tony when he's whistling!). Another is its boldness in not cutting away when someone gets shot up. While the violence in this film is no where near as graphic as that you see today (its '83 counterpart included) it was very controversial for the time and I would argue still goes a long way in showing how wild and violent the days of prohibition really were. And of course, what good would a gangster film be if you didn't have the right actor for the job and on that note, Paul Muni delivers. With his zeal and energy, Muni dives right into the part of Tony, becoming him even, and forever ingraining in our minds one of the most ruthless gangsters in cinema ever. It also helps that writer Ben Hecht was able to draw on his experiences of a Chicago reporter during the 1920's and as such, some of the events and characters in the film will appear very familiar to those who know their Chicago gang history (The big fellow himself, Al Capone, was said to love this film so much he had his own print of it, probably because he saw a lot of himself in Tony!). But above all, the film still packs a punch due to the fact that despite the number of years since this picture was made, there are still plenty of would be Tonys out there looking to get their hands on the American dream, regardless of the cost. So with all that said, find a comfortable chair, close the shades, and check out this American classic. Just be careful of the Tommy guns though! Also starring George Raft, Ann Dovak, and before he was Frankenstein's monster, Boris Karloff.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Classic film about the rise and fall of Tony Camonte (Paul Muni), a violent gangster with an unhealthy relationship with his younger sister Cesca (Ann Dvorak). Tony shoots his way to the top of the underworld but when he discovers his best friend (George Raft) is having a relationship with Cesca, he loses control and everything begins to unravel.One of the trio of gangster classics, along with Little Caesar and Public Enemy, that defined the genre. This is the only one of the three that wasn't made by Warner Bros. It cries out for a music score at times, as early talkies often do. But Howard Hawks' excellent direction, a first-rate script by Ben Hecht, and some dynamic acting keeps you from ever getting bored. Great turns from Paul Muni and Ann Dvorak. First big role for George Raft, who does a fine job. Boris Karloff has a fun part as a rival gangster. Vince Barnett provides the movie's only laughs as Tony's secretary who has a humorous way of taking messages. A wonderful, exciting film that's a must-see for fans of gangster movies. Hard to compare to the popular 1983 remake, given the decades separating the two. I would recommend them both, though.</fullReview>

		<date>2010-01-16</date>
		<username>Bill Slocum (bill.slocum@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Firstest With The Mostest</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Paul Muni wants to take you for a ride. You better get in.Muni is Tony Camonte, a scarfaced goon from the tough Five Points section of New York who comes to Chicago with his coin-flipping comrade Guino (George Raft) for torpedo work with a top crime boss. Next thing you know, he's making eyes for the boss's moll Poppy (Karen Morley) and the North Side racket run by a rival gang. Everything's roses for Tony, at least until his sister Cesca (Ann Dvorak) finds herself a guy...I think you could have a long bull session with '30s film fans talking up which of the "Big Three" early gangster films was the best. "Scarface" came out later than the other two films, "Little Caesar" and "Public Enemy", but was made at the same time. What held it up was the fact it hit much closer to the bone, using the real story of Al "Scarface" Capone" to underscore its plot and pushing sex and violence onto the screen in a way big studios would avoid for decades.The most important argument in "Scarface's" favor is Muni. That despite the fact the stars of the other two films are far better known and had more interesting careers. Muni's performance is riveting, a mixture of tough-guy bravado, merry quips, and bat-guano crazy. His first scene sets the tone, when he sizes up a scowling lawman who has come to take him in - then lights his match on the cop's badge.The neon sign across the street from Tony's apartment drives him on. "The World Is Yours" it reads. "Someday I look at that sign and I say: 'Okay, she's mine.'" Camonte's smugness as he says this sells it; we're right there with Poppy loving and hating him at the same time.The film bends over backwards to present a social message that I think is genuinely meant but rather ham-handedly delivered, especially in one scene by a newspaper editor who looks as if he had a few before shooting. The comedy relief is weaker than it should be; too much of Tony's flunky Angelo (Vince Barnett) having trouble answering a telephone because of his poor English.But the film doesn't leave you a lot of time to dwell on its flaws. With Howard Hawks at his best as an action-film director, "Scarface" zips from setpiece to setpiece. One moment Tony's at a dance slugging a guy for dancing too close to his sister. Then he's in an armor-plated sedan chased by tommy-gunners in a roadster. Then he's confronting his boss about a set-up. Ninety-three minutes fly by, but never feel rushed. Writer Ben Hecht knew the gangster world, and fills in the lulls with dialogue that still sounds fresh and real."Costillo slowed down too much, yeah. And now he come to a dead stop."About that sister... I'm not sure it's exactly incestuous feelings that drives Tony as much as his possessiveness. He's too buggy a guy to let others do what they want, because all he knows is what he wants. It's a fatal flaw, and plays out in a finale that perversely celebrates the bond between Tony and Cesca. "You're me, and I'm you," she says. "It's always been that way."Given how gangster films come and go, it's perhaps not strange how the earliest ones are among the most enduring. "Scarface" stands comfortably on its pedestal 80 years on.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2009-09-24</date>
		<username>classicsoncall</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>""Do it first, do it yourself, and keep on doin' it!"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Along with Edward G. Robinson's "Little Caesar" and Jimmy Cagney's "The Public Enemy", "Scarface" helped usher in the big time gangster era in film of the early 1930's. Universal Pictures took a page from the Warner brothers play book with an opening indictment of gang rule and crime in America, slamming the government's seeming indifference and inability to stop it. The picture itself is a fast paced and brutal affair, accentuated by the rapid staccato machine gun fire set to a flourish of riffling calendar dates, a very nice touch. By contrast, Paul Muni's first appearance as mobster Tony Camonte is appropriately low key, emerging from beneath the hot towels of a neighborhood barber shop. I found it a bit unusual actually, that Camonte 'took it' from Detective Guarino (C. Henry Gordon) in that brief encounter; I was waiting the rest of the picture to see if Tony got a measure of personal revenge.There was another scene that caught my eye, primarily in it's presaging a similar situation in 1972's "The Godfather". When Tony and pal Guino Rinaldi (George Raft) come to learn that gang rival Meehan wasn't killed in a mob ambush, they go back to the hospital to finish the job. That was interesting from another perspective as well, highlighting as it does the virtual impossibility of era newspapers to cover events in real time the way they often did in film. By the time Camonte explains to Johnny Lovo that Meehan and Bardini were hit 'an hour ago', it was already a headline!I was curious enough about some of the reviews on this board regarding Tony's relationship with his sister that I went back and watched the picture again immediately the next day. For those insisting on something more going on there, I'm just not buying it. To understand, I think you'd have to have known or been friends with a hot headed Italian like the college roommate I had. If he was any kind of example, one would know that Italian men are fiercely protective of family, defending mother and sisters with equal abandon. Camonte's obsession with protecting Cesca (Ann Dvorak) had all to do with his personal code of defending her honor against all men, since he knew what they were capable of. Besides, he couldn't view Cesca the same way he himself could look at Poppy (Karen Morley), someone he could relate to at some street level kind of way.Some other observations - I couldn't help notice the resemblance between Paul Muni's characterization here with that of Charles Bronson - both had a similar build to go with their mannerisms. It was also cool to see Boris Karloff in a gangster role, even if he didn't last very long in the story.Interestingly, the DVD presentation of "Scarface" I viewed also offered the alternate ending in which Tony was captured instead of being gunned down. Because the scenes were filmed after the movie was already completed, Paul Muni doesn't appear in the scenes where Camonte is found guilty by a jury and is sentenced to death for his crimes. Had that been the official theater release, I think the film might not have survived with the same reputation it has now. There's no replacing the inherent idea that a top shelf movie mobster has to go out in a blaze of glory.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>chrisj893</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Hughs' Direction Shines</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Scarface: The Shame of the Nation, directed by Howard Hughs is an excellent film that depicts so well the evolution of film from the silents of the mid-twenties to the sound films of the 30s. It is apparent that Hughs was simply ahead of his time as a director. He creates sequences with very few cuts and a moving camera, something that Hollywood directors weren't doing at the time. Perhaps it had to do with Hughs' OCD disorder, or his strive for constant perfection, but the direction Scarface stands out like no other. The acting in Scarface also stands out, Paul Muni and Ann Dvorak help the film along beautifully. The acting and direction is so well done in this film that it could be a silent one and the message would have come across just as well. One criticism would be the lighting used throughout the movie, it seems that with all the 'fancy' new technology of the day and sound, Hughs forgot about a key element of film making, lighting. In other films like, Sunrise, for example, the director (F.W. Murnau) isn't burdened with the idea of sound, therefore he focused more on lighting, cinematography and film effects. For moments in the film I felt as though Hughs got carried away with the idea of making a blockbuster film with full sound. Otherwise Scarface is a brilliant film, Hughs brings a real world feel into the theater with solid cinematography and long, moving shots. The direction is what makes this film shine as one of the best of the thirties.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Emblematic work of the gangster film and noir cinema of all time.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Film directed by Howard Hawks movie with Richard Rosson, is the film adaptation of the novel "Scarface" (1930) by Armitage Trail (pseudonym of Maurice Coons). It was filmed in the United Artists Studios (Hollywood). Produced by Howard Hughes and Howard Hawks, premiered on 9-IV-1932.The action takes place in Chicago between 1928 and 1930. It tells the story of the rise and fall of mobster "Tony Camonte" (Paul Muni), an Italian immigrant, came to America in 1920 with his parents and younger sister. Becomes part of the criminal gang "Luigi Costillo," which becomes her bodyguard. After killing him coldly, his new boss, "Johnny Lovo" (Osgood Perkins) lifts the enormous envy the luxury of your home and the beauty of his mistress, "Poppy" (Karen Moley). After the murder of "Lovo" and other crime bosses, became the master of Chicago, until the urge to dominate his sister "Francesca" (Ann Dvorak) cause its decline and downfall.It is the paradigm of the genre of gangster, ranks among the top 3 productions from the golden age of the genre (early 30), along with "Little Caesar" (1931) and "The Public Enemy" (1931). Possibly the best of 3. It contains many facts related to the figure of Al Capone, as the nickname "Scarface", the slaughter of Valentine's Day (14-II-1929), the murder of Big Jim Colosino and others, but not his biopic. "Tony" run a terrible race of murder, driven by ambition to get money and power in the city of crime and corruption, which marks a qualitative leap in the use of consistent automatic rifles, with a capacity of 300 rounds per minute . The ancient incidents become selective massacres, running with impunity from cars running the race. The work, extreme violence, including between 26 and 28 murders, a censure under more tolerant than that applied from 1934 (Hayes Code). "Tony" contemptible and fascinating at the same time, power-hungry and eager for wealth and domination, has similarities with "Macbeth." Compulsive exercising control over the sister has a hint of incestuous love. It was the subject of a "remake", "Scarface" (1983).The music combines jazz, dance melodies and environmental clubs, with a beautiful song sung by a soloist. Photography, eminently expressionist creates images of great beauty in composition and drawing. It makes use of superb sequence shots, frequent ellipsis and off-field scenes, reinforcing the tragic dimension of the story. The script adds to violence strokes black humor sometimes and occasionally macabre, supported largely by the figure of the secretary "Angelo". The interpretation of Paul Muni is possibly the best of his career. The resource management creates the precarious 1931/32 highlight film.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>In a Chicago-styled city, bootlegging Paul Muni (Antonio "Tony" Camonte) shoots his way to the top of the underworld. It worked out well for those other two gangsters that producer Howard Hughes and director Howard Hawks had trouble getting "Scarface" distributed, because it blew them out of the water by intensely raising the level of violence depicted in a crime drama. The sound of machine guns had never been so artfully communicated.These films can be said to romanticize and promote the "gangster lifestyle" - no matter what they do to script an "appropriate" and/or "moralistic" ending, a dynamic lead performance (Cagney, Muni, or Robinson) makes the villain heroic. Also raising eyebrows was the hint of an incestuous interest Mr. Muni's character has for his teenage sister Ann Dvorak (as Francesca "Cesca" Camonte); however, these kind of sexual subtexts (both intentional and not) were nothing new.Additionally fine supporting performances are delivered by banged-up mob boss Osgood Perkins (as John "Johnny" Lovo), henchman George Raft (as Guino "Little Boy" Rinaldo), leggy blonde Karen Morley (as "Poppy"), inept male secretary Vince Barnett (as Angelo), and bowling rival Boris Karloff (as Gaffney). Screen-player Ben Hecht and the writers adapt Armitage Trail well."Scarface" made the annual ten best lists complied by the "National Board of Review" and "Film Daily" but was overlooked by "Academy Award" voters. At the time, neither Howard (Hughes or Hawks) was very popular with "Oscar" givers, who were encouraged to vote for studio favorites; "Scarface" was independently produced. Muni was very popular, and received an "Best Actor" nomination for his follow-up "I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang" produced for Hal B. 		<shortDescription>Original Gangster</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The original scarface that i had no idea even existed... Today Al Pacinos version is highly acclaimed and has been blown up to all proportions. Any inspiring thug or drug dealer looks to that movie with giant gleaming eyes. Pacinos Scarface glorifies Italians and their mob mentalities, exposing extreme murder, drug use, and corruption. The 1932 version tells the tale of somewhat exaggerated truth. During that time period gangsters were frowned upon by the government but glorified by the public. Gangsters captured the publics attention due to their heroic efforts in taking what they thought they were entitled to, even if they had to kill for it. This film highlights a lot of the present day activity during that time period. The 1930's were an extremely hard time for America and crime ran rampant. Gangster films were extremely popular. Scarface was the king of them all, but unfortunately the government at the time didn't want the public exposed to such crude and vulgar acts carried out by the skilled gangster actors. The film was held up for two years while restrictions were slapped on it. Many states even banned the film. This film was great in my opinion. I personally am a big fan of the gangsters of the 1930's. I think they stood up for what they believed in, they were criminals but were also hero's. They carried out acts organists banks and such that the average American struggling during that time would love to do. The film itself has a great deal of action, suspense, and crafty acting. I think its fun to take a look into history and this film portrays a great piece of American history. This film was action packed, much like what you'll find in a lot of the mainstream movies of today. Interesting that with all the murders portrayed in the film and there was no blood shed... Censorship i take it. We have come a long way in that aspect. There isn't much censorship going on in todays action films. Violence has reached its absolute peak, and i don't believe it can be taken to a higher. Scarface (1932 ) I believe showed Americans fascination with violence and crime. I think the movie displays Americas affection for the " bad guy " and our civil duty to rebel against to authority. Would certainly suggest this film.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>One of the first talking gangster films and a cinematic landmark</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film is based on real events that happened in the criminal career of Al Capone, although Capone's criminal career had already ended with his conviction on charges of tax evasion six months before this film was released in April 1932. You know you're watching a Howard Hughes production when, during the first scene, a bar employee is sweeping up after a party held by one of Chicago's big gangsters and finds a bra among the confetti. The film shares some aspects with its gangster film predecessors - Tony Camonte is motivated by a desire for power just as Edward G. Robinson's Rico was in "Little Caesar", and also like Rico takes over the gang from a boss he perceives as weak. However, Camonte doesn't seem to have the pent-up rage of Public Enemy's Tom Powers. When Tony performs acts of violence it is usually related to gangland business. The actual deaths are strictly business, but the execution of the killings themselves are something Tony takes pride in - a sort of work of art on his part.Like Tom Powers, Tony Camonte is given a family background, but unlike Tom Powers, Camonte's family is a completely dysfunctional one. What is unique in this gangster picture is Tony's trio of love interests. He wants his boss' girl, Poppy, as a status symbol. He also seems to have a love affair going with the machine gun, acting like he has discovered America the first time he shoots one. Finally, Tony is in love with his own sister Cesca. Tony's only true fits of rage occur when he sees her with another man, and it is this loss of emotional control over this one issue that is ultimately his downfall. George Raft, an ex-gangster of sorts himself, is terrific as the smart and level-headed Guino Rinaldo, Tony's right-hand man. Finally there is Vince Barnett as Tony's extremely inadequate secretary in a bit of comic relief turned tragic at the end of the film.</fullReview>

Whatever was missing in the remake, was present in this 1932 classic.The story is based loosely on the criminal career of Prohibition-Era Mafia kingpin, Alphonse Capone (who's nick-name was Scarface) and a novel of the same name by Armitage Trail. The movie chronicles the rise and fall of Antonio "Tony" Camonte (Paul Muni), who begins to elevate his role in the Chicago mob. He gets a crew together (including George Raft) and dominates the city. His line of work causes family tension (with Ann Dvorak as his sister, Cesca) and trouble with the cops and rival gangs (including an appearance by the celebrated Boris Karloff as Irish mob boss, Gaffney). Soon, he becomes the cities most powerful crime boss and suffers a dramatic fall from grace.With all honesty, this version was better than the remake with Al Pacino. All major plot-points from the remake still exist in this one (his love/hate relationship with his sister, his love-affair with his boss's lover, and the main character's rise and fall of power). Paul Muni deliver's a great performance as Camonte, the rest of the cast also done a fine job. This movie blew me away, while the remake did as well but not to the degree that this classic piece of film-noir did. I love them both, but I have to point my fingers to this one as the better movie. This is easily one of the greatest gangster films of all time.10/10</fullReview>

And Anne Dvorak is very good as Tony's sister, whose relationship raises incestuous questions.What most impressed me was how Hawks was able to stage such beautifully shocking sequences of violence. Of course they are so timid for today, but it is the lack of visuals that lead to stronger feelings. And it all leads up to a fantastic conclusion with a message pointed right at you.</fullReview>

		<username>lugonian</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>King of the Underworld</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SCARFACE (United Artists, 1932), directed by Howard Hawks, ranks one of the finer crime stories ever produced during the gangster cycle of the early 1930s. Inspired by the recent successes of Warners' own LITTLE CAESAR (1930) and THE PUBLIC ENEMY (1931), which made overnight successes of Edward G. Robinson and James Cagney respectively, SCARFACE in turn helped gained recognition for two upcoming young actors, that of Paul Muni, having already been featured in two forgotten 1929 Fox films, THE VALIANT and SEVEN FACES, and George Raft. Unlike Robinson, Cagney, Raft and later Humphrey Bogart, Muni never lived up to his reputation as a movie gangster, having never enacted the same type of character twice. Although he did play a gangster again, in a comedy fantasy of ANGEL ON MY SHOULDER (United Artists, 1946), nothing remained as powerful nor as memorable as his role of the Al Capone-inspired Tony Camonte. In spite of the cross-like mark engraved on the left side of his face, he's never once referred to on screen as the title role.FORWARD: "This picture is an indictment of gang rule in America and of the callous indifference of the government to this constantly increasing menace to our society and our liberty. Every incident in this picture is the reproduction of an actual occurrence, and the purpose of this picture is to demand of the government, 'What are you going to do about it?' The government is your government. What are YOU going to do about it?" With all that said, the story gets underway on the 22nd Street section of Chicago with the shooting of Louie Costillo (Henry J. Vejar), "the last of the old-fashioned gang leaders" following all night party, by a whistling dark shadow entering the empty restaurant. The prime suspect is Tony Camonte (Paul Muni), a tough punk with a cross scar on the left side of his face, who got his start as Costillo's bodyguard, and now working for rival crime boss Johnny Lovo (Osgood Perkins). Assisted by his right-hand man, Guido Rinaldo (George Raft), Camonte, whose mother (Inez Palage) claims him to be "no good," and hated by his 18-year-old sister, Cesca (Ann Dvorak) for his over-protective ways, rises to the top of his profession by stepping into territories of rival gangsters on the North side, doing away with them massacre style, taking over Lovo's establishment as well as acquiring his mistress, Poppy (Karen Morley), for his very own. In spite of being constantly hounded by detective Ben Guarino (C. Henry Gordon), Tony's life of crime would eventually reach its pinnacle point after "taking care of the guy" who married his kid sister. With other crime movies of the day, SCARFACE makes every attempt in topping them all, even those produced by Warner Brothers, where many of the best gangster films were made. Muni, Dvorak and later Raft would become contract players at the studio, which could cause SCARFACE to easily be mistaken as a Warners film. Boris Karloff comes as an even bigger surprise seen listed in the cast as Gaffney, a rival gang boss, whose key scene comes in a bowling alley where he strikes his last game. Other members of the cast include Tully Marshall (The Managing Editor); Edwin Maxwell (The Chief of Detectives); and Purnell B. Pratt (Garston). Vince Barnett as Angelo, Tony's secretary, is the only character in the story who enacts his role in comedic fashion, which somewhat unbalances the story as a whole. Barnett attempts an Italian accent while Muni's accent is far from being an imitation Chico Marx, but remains a strong central character regardless. Dvorak, however, looks quite different from her subsequent film roles sporting dark curly hair and heavy makeup, which adds to the character, but her serious overtone and screeching after the shooting of her husband is overly melodramatic that would stir up laughter for contemporary audiences, but in general as "a young girl with grown up ideas" chasing after men is perfect casting. Karen Morley, whether intentional or not, starts off as being stiff as the gangster's moll, but improves as the movie progresses. George Raft's signature role as Camonte's coin flipping trustee assistant is certainly one that gained him recognition as well as a studio contract for Paramount (1932-1939) shortly after this film's release.Rarely seen since its initial theatrical release, SCARFACE eventually turned up on cable television during its early cycle of the 1980s on Wometco Home Theater before airing frequently on American Movie Classics (1989-90) and Turner Classic Movies (starting in 2001). It was also one of the first films from the classic movie era to be distributed to home video by about the same time as the 1983 remake starring Al Pacino in a remake brought up to date, changing the Tony character from Italian mobster of Chicago to Cuban refugee in Florida, containing more intense violence that the original ever allowed at that time, each concluding with the neon sign reading, "The World is Yours," along with a closing dedication to director Howard Hawks after its extreme three hour length. As for the original, which was reportedly first previewed at 99 minutes, circulating prints are currently available at 92 minutes. In closing, Paul Muni gives a masterful performance in the title role, but his next role, I AM A FUGITIVE FROM A CHAIN GANG (Warners, 1932) is even better. (***)</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Movie Odyssey Review #062: Scarface</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>062: Scarface (1932) - released 3/31/1932; viewed 6/5/06.KEVIN: Scarface rounds out the gangster trilogy that included Little Caesar and The Public Enemy. I still like Public Enemy the best, but this movie has some great direction from Howard Hawks. I caught the business with the "X" signaling death the first time I watched it, and even more here. When Boris Karloff's Gaffney gets a strike at the bowling alley, the writing's on his scorecard. The most notable thing about Scarface is that apparently as the violence in society escalated and increased, so did the violence in the films that depicted it (and not the other way around, as many conservative groups have often claimed). I'd forgotten that there were so many on-screen deaths since the first time I'd seen the movie, more than in the other film's we've watched from the past year. Another of the differences between this and the other two gangster classics is that Scarface kind of comes in at the middle of Tony's rise to the top, where Caesar and Enemy are both biographies of their main characters. The scenes when Tony and his fellow torpedoes are going around marketing their booze is a faster version of what we saw Tom Powers &amp; Co. doing in Enemy. And the bloody climax is cooler than the final shootout in Caesar. One might notice in this review how much I'm comparing this film to other movies, and that is an obstacle this movie must overcome, which it does. It's not the best gangster picture, but it stands on its own as the most graphic and realistic depiction yet of things that were going on at the time every day.DOUG: We watched this film about three years ago in Action Adventure class. Since then we've watched Brian De Palma's 1983 remake with Al Pacino. We had to tape it on TCM, since it's only available on DVD as part of the big Scarface gift set (gold money clip and all). Now here on the Odyssey, it completes the classic gangster movie trilogy with Little Caesar and The Public Enemy. Howard Hawks flexes his muscles as the best director of genre pictures the Studio Era had ever seen. Edward G. Robinson and James Cagney create more vivid characters, but Scarface is probably the most relevant film dealing with the problem of organized crime in its day, not to mention introducing us to the most dangerous gangster anti-hero of all, Tony Camonte. Paul Muni plays Camonte to perfection, a volatile hothead who feels he must prove himself through violence to move up the ladder. I'm still scratching my head as to why the great Paul Muni wasn't in more movies. Maybe it was his heart problems. At first Tony seems content to take orders from his boss, Johnny Lovo (Osgood Perkins), until he discovers the Tommy Gun. My favorite scene will always be when Tony storms into Johnny's office, Tommy in hand, and declares that he will use his new "typewriter" to "write his name all over town in big letters!" A fire lights up in his eyes as his ambitions soar; he takes orders from no one now. I knew it was violent when I saw it the first time; now I can see that it's even more violent than its predecessors. It's also more conscious of its message, with the opening credits blaring it for all to see. We also get a scene where the police chief and the newspaper bigwigs sit around and debate what to do about all these gangsters, essentially calling to the audience to take action.Last film: Tarzan the Ape Man (1932). Next film: Grand Hotel (1932).</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>abgkasjlkasjla</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Raised the bar for all later gangster films</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is the first Howard Hawks film that I've seen... and if they're all this good, I'll be sure to watch all of them. This movie really impressed me... I had not expected so much from such an old film. This is excellent film noir... the shadows, the dialog, the tone... while it didn't entertain me quite as much as The Big Sleep with Bogie himself(a hard feat to do, even for another noir), it's still high up there on the list of movies that pretty much any man(as in race, not gender) should see, in particular any that would claim him or herself a fan of noir and crime films. The plot is great, and it develops very nicely throughout. The pacing is solid, I was never bored. The acting is top-notch, everyone hands in a credible performance. The characters are well-written. The editing is magnificent, with some nice stylistic choices that surpass most anything else of the period. The cinematography, I think, impressed me the most; long, panning shots and angles that I would never have guessed film-makers were capable of so long ago. This film is almost 75 years old, yet it easily beats new releases. Now, it's impossible to watch this film without making comparisons to the '83 remake(or the remake without making comparisons to this; something I didn't realize when I watched the Pacino version). I will say that the similarities are plentiful, and I'd say that in most cases, this is the better film(not counting the many changes and additions of De Palma's version), but the film that Oliver Stone wrote still has the upper hand in a few of these scenes. The idea also seems somewhat grander, larger-scale in the newer film... whether that's a good thing or not is up to the viewer to decide, but with the second film showing a longer progression and having Tony start lower, the story does become bigger. With that said, this definitely has better pacing, dialog and all-round acting. This has the style that the latter film tries to make up for lacking by instead going for more violence. For its time, this was violent... it certainly pushed the envelope, but by today's standards, it seems tame. No matter; if you're watching this film for the sake of violence, you're missing the point and you've got the wrong movie, anyway. One character in this movie truly irritated me... he was inconceivably, uncontrollably(though, as my father pointed out when we watched this, rather convincingly) stupid. So much supposed "humor"(which the film has in spades, and of a fairly high quality) is drawn from his obnoxious role, making it abundantly clear to anyone that this film was made in the era of such characteristically unfunny comedy teams as Laurel and Hardy and The Three Stooges, which were all based on that very same kind of idea of passing intense stupidity as comedy. The thing that bothered me more, however, was the blatant, insultingly obvious message of the film. Sure, it's a good message(if a bit close-minded as well as black and white), but why shove it down our faces like that? It doesn't take a genius to realize it; crime is *bad*. Organized crime: guess what, even worse. Surely, one would think, that even in the time of these mobsters, people would read the papers, hear about what was going on and be smart enough to realize that? I disagree with just about every "law" person in the film, and their view on criminals, making every single scene containing them(which, compared to the full running time, isn't terribly many) somewhat cringe-inducing... mostly because they're practically speaking to 'us', to the viewers, as if to provoke a reaction. For such a high point in cinema, it's sad to think that this film may truly have been conceived and created with the sole object of scaring people away from criminals... something the acts of them(the gangsters) ought to do quite well by themselves. But I digress. This is a highly enjoyable, if just slightly propagandistic, film that will satisfy the need for noir, great acting and smart dialog in any viewer, young or old. I recommend this to any fan of anyone involved in making it and every fan of noir. 8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>KARLOFF HAS A DEADLY BOWLING GAME!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Scarface" 1932 is truly is a great film Classic, directed by Howard Hawks. It takes place during the prohibition days, Johnny Lovo(Osgood Perkins), a Chicago gangster, tries to take control over the beer business in his territory along with the aide of another gangster, Tony Camonte(Paul Muni). Camonte does Lovo a favor and rubs out his boss and gains trust and support in the gang. Camonte becomes head strong and even steals Lovo's mistress Poppy(Karen Morley). Eventually Camonte wants control of the North Side of Chicago and machine-guns his entire gang. Gaffney (Boris Karloff) another dangerous mobster is killed while enjoying a great game of bowling. Ann Dvorak (Cesca) and even Rinaldo(George Raft) appear in this classic. This film was adapted from Armitage Trail's novel, and is really about the career of Al Capone and the reinactment from Chicago's crime-filled history like the killing of "Big Jim" Colosimo, the St. Valentine's Day Massacre. 		<shortDescription>One of the Classic Gangster Films...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Back in the 1930s, there were a lot of amazing gangster films coming out. Films like "Little Caesar", "The Public Enemy", and, this film, "Scarface".Directed by the always great Howard Hawks, "Scarface" is one of the least dated films of the 1930s, and still manages to hold up very well. I cannot compare it to its remake, because I haven't seen the remake, but I believe that if you have seen the remake, you shouldn't base your thoughts on this film off of that, since the two films were made in two separate decades (this one being from the 30's, and the Brian DePalma directed version being made in the 80's) and both have very different runtimes. The remake is literally over an hour longer than this version! Obviously, there must be quite a few differences between these films.I'm kind of glad I couldn't base my thoughts of this original version off of the 80's version, because I can fully see how much it manages to work as a stand alone film. With some astounding action based sequences, compelling drama, and some very interesting and artistic direction. Hawks uses various creative techniques, some involving shadows, which give the film a more menacing and dark touch, making it feel less like some violent action movie (which it manages to have many elements of) and more like a Film Noir.The acting is pretty good, Paul Muni makes the character both extremely hatable and pretty sympathetic at times, and Boris Karloff (famous for being in a lot of classic horror films such as "Frankenstein" and "The Mummy") shows up, and is pretty great, too!I cannot come up with any negative things about the film. It is far more than just a racy, controversial 30's movie, and doesn't deserve to forever be categorized as the "original 'Scarface' movie".</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The World is Yours</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Scarface" is one of my favorite and one of the best gangster film plots in the history of cinema. A single man, Tony Camote, rising to power from his lesser position of society truly embellishes the American Dream's darker path to success. Scarface was a successful film targeted at mainstream audiences of the time and geared towards a very real and despised gangster industry that had swept through the nation. It was a masterpiece to watch, as well as Brian de Palma's remake. They are truly supplemental and if you've seen one, you should see the other as well. Scarface remains one of my favorite old gangster crime dramas for its brilliant depiction of the life of gangsters in 1930's Chicago. It is a roller coaster ride of betrayal, revenge, and love that is action packed from start to finish. Humor also drives the characters such as Tony and especially his secretary who's humor further adds to the cinematic masterpiece of Howard Hawks and Richard Rosson. Paul Muni brings a certain depth to an anti hero that the audience finds themselves rooting for despite his illegal intentions. The audience wants to see him accomplish the American Dream as well as see him stopped due to the nature of the film that was both socially conscious and genuine about the world of 1932. The audience back then saw these shootouts and gangsters in the big cities and this movie portrayed the openness and fearlessness in which these gangsters operated well. One powerful and recurring element of the story is the quote "The World is Yours" which Tony adopts as a sign to take what is rightfully "his" and, to me, that was very interesting and symbolic throughout. The action is packed into this 93 minute film and always around every corner. The plot is one of my favorite gangster plots and remains to be rivaled to this day. A man rising to power and achieving his goal of "The American Dream" through illegal means and facing the music for his pride and greed. The ending, which i will not reveal, is poignant as well as thrilling and it had me on the edge of my seat rather than the alternate ending, spawning due to censorship issues of the time, which just doesn't do the movie justice. This film was subsequently influential to American Cinema and spawned a brilliant remake and soon will spawn another Scarface title in the next few years due to its brilliant plot and acting as well as its social and historical significance.</fullReview>

		<username>Scott LeBrun (Hey_Sweden)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>"Do it first, do it yourself, and keep on doing it."</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One of a handful of 1930s favourites that very much came to define the gangster picture, "Scarface" is a stark, grim, and effectively violent film, a solid collaboration between producer Howard Hughes and (co) director Howard Hawks. The splendid Paul Muni is a force of nature as an Al Capone-inspired mobster, Tony Camonte. Although ostensibly working for people like Johnny Lovo (Osgood Perkins), Tony has his own way of doing things. He's ruthless, ambitious, and impulsive; he has no problem with doing the dirty work. He also tends to get very touchy when it comes to the matter of his kid sister Cesca (Ann Dvorak).Good times are ahead when you see gangster specialist George Raft is playing Tony's associate Rinaldo, and especially when the wonderful Boris Karloff turns up, in a substantial non-horror role, as a competing mafia goon named Tom Gaffney. Both Dvorak, and Karen Morley as Poppy, the gal who catches Tony's eye, are striking and appealing talents. C. Henry Gordon squints and snarls entertainingly as Inspector Guarino, the lawman just itching to take down our flamboyant antihero. But it's Muni who will command most of the attention. He's got a fun and meaty role, and really makes the most of it."Scarface" is vivid, well paced, well edited, and atmospheric. Although crime films in the decades to come would up the ante in terms of splatter and sadism, the violence in this was pretty shocking for its time. Bullets fly on a constant basis, and bodies drop. "Scarface" even begins with a nicely staged murder set piece.Even though films like "Scarface" would get accused of glorifying underworld life, this one clearly has a different agenda in mind, going so far as to denounce organized crime in its opening minutes. Still, it was a tough sell for Hughes, who eventually made the bold move of giving "Scarface" wide releases only in those states without strict censorship boards.Powerful stuff overall, with an engaging and rousing big finale.Seven out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

At one point, the infamous Valentine's Day Massacre occurs, where Tony leads his men, disguised as police officers, and mows down his rivals in a garage. His friend Johnny, the head of the gang, eventually grows weary of Tony and his rise to prominence, and demands Tony to be killed. Tony overcomes the assassination attempt, and lives up to the billboard outside of his apartment: The World Is Yours. Tony ultimately meets the long arm of the law, and winds up on the wrong end of a pointed gun, proving that crime doesn't pay. I feel that this is an absolutely wonderful film, where the Hays Code was disregarded for great reasons. 		<username>Michael Rice</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>It's good for what it is</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Scarface, among other things, is a gangster flick that takes place during the prohibition era and is loosely based on Al Capone. It focuses on being as gritty and as lifelike as possible, given the time it was made this was quite a feat having various censorship codes to hurdle over. I was quite surprised to see some of the shootouts and drive-byes in Scarface, not because they were particularly graphic or stunning, but simply because it was allowed in a movie made in 1932. Scarface follows Tony Camonte, a hostile gangster willing to murder his way to the top, as he murders his way to the top. Along the blood path we find his love interest, his sister whom Tony cares deeply for, and an assortment of gangsters and policemen. All the elements needed for a good mobster film. The problem with Scarface is that it isn't very engaging. The isn't really any effort put in for you to like Tony Camonte or some of the other characters , with the exception of Cesca. It's a gritty film capitalizing on the drama and action of the gangsters of the time, but it isn't as well developed as say, Godfather. Given how it was made and Scarface's censor hurdles, it's still quite a good film. It wouldn't be the first gangster/mobster film I'd see if given a choice (even picking the remake over the original), but Scarface is still worth watching.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Scarface</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In Howard Hawks and Richard Rosson's Scarface (1932), Johnny Lovo (Osgood Perkins) decides to hire Tony Camonte (Paul Muni), the bodyguard to Louis Costillo, head of the bootlegging crime scene so that Lovo can take his place at the top. In doing so he takes Tony as his second in command and personal bodyguard. This backfires for Lovo, though, when Tony stops following Lovo's orders and starts making his own decisions, unafraid of the the gang on the north side of Chicago lead by O'Hara, an Irish man whom Lovo is afraid of. As Tony's murder count climbs higher and higher, he and Lovo realize that they have different ideas as to what their ideal world would be. That and the fact that Tony is attracted to Lovo's girlfriend, Poppy. Can Tony and Johnny Lovo coexist in the same world? Can Lovo hang on to his girl? ANd most importantly, can Tony protect his younger sister who is a lot more like him than he is comfortable with? Definitely a must see!</fullReview>

Also, it was kind of shocking how much gun violence their was in this movie, considering when it was made. But what sets Scarface apart from other gangster movies is its influential narrative. The classic gangster narrative thats used in almost every gangster movie i can think of (with the exception of The Godfather) is refined in this movie. A young hood works his way through the ranks to become a big shoot, but the power becomes too much and he eventually falls from power. This is the story of Tony, and Michael Corleone, and Henry Hill, and Tony Montana, and Stringer Bell, and Avon Barksdale, and pretty much any other gangster i can think of. Without this movie, none of those other movies would even exist. 8/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Very Much To the Point</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Immediately following "Scarface"'s opening credits and before the film even begins, there is a sort of public service announcement pleading for the viewer to stand up against gang crime, which was on the rise at the time the film was released. This disclaimer sets the tone for the rest of the film, but the two don't quite jive. It brought to mind Upton Sinclair's novel "The Jungle" about the mistreatment of immigrant workers, and I asked myself why the filmmakers who worked on "Scarface" couldn't have pushed their anti-gang agenda with a little more subtlety or finesse. That being said, the film is very well made. The cinematography in particular caught my eye. Many shots in the film are long, slow pans that allow the viewer to focus on the actors' performances and the set designs without being too distracting. The sound effects during the action sequences. The film is produced by Howard Hughes, and it seems to embody his reckless, larger-than-life attitude. Overall, it was very entertaining and interesting to see how differently crime was depicted on screen nearly a century ago.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Great classic gangster movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Scarface is a 1932 gangster movie directed by Howard Hawks and starring Paul Muni . During the initial movie credits, the viewer is informed that the story is based on true events, primarily the exploits of Al Capone. There is also a plea to the movie viewer to take action, and support the government efforts in the fight against gangsters and the crime-wave. Set in the early 1930's, the movie opens with a long, continuous shot focusing on a group of gangsters conversing after a party. While not overt, the gangsters are stereotyped to be of Italian descent, with 'Big Louie' as the boss. Using shadows rather than the actual appearance of actors, the suspense builds as Louie is followed and eventually murdered after the party. The plot then centers on the rise of Tony Camonte (Muni) to become a crime boss. And while Tony craves the respect of his men and girlfriend, he only holds power through fear and intimidation. The conflict Tony faces throughout the movie is his desire to keep his younger sister free of amoral behavior. In the end, he observes the tragic demise of his sister in her efforts to emulate him, and this eventually leads to his own destruction.</fullReview>

		<date>2013-01-12</date>
		<username>ElMaruecan82</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Scarface: the Shame of a Moralizing Code</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's easy to assume that Howard Hawks' "Scarface" is a grander film than De Palma's 'flashy' 80's remake, on the simple basis that it's the original one, but let's not jump to such a quick conclusion, several acclaimed masterpieces can be tagged as 'remakes': "The Wizard of Oz", "The Maltese Falcon", "The Ten Commandments", "Ben-Hur" etc which I hope would dismiss the whole 'original superior to remake' argument.Having said that, there is no doubt that Howard Hawks' "Scarface" is a cinematic landmark constituting with "Little Caesar" and "The Public Enemy", the seminal Trinity that defined the gangster genre, probably the most prevalent genre on the long term. Movies that had the gutsy merit to create unforgettable characters who were not the good guys, and that's why the genre was ahead of its time, it paved the way for the most popular cinematic figure: the antihero, from Caesar Enrico 'Rico' Bandallo aka "Little Caesar", to Tom Powers aka "The Public Enemy" and last but not least, Tony Camonte aka "Scarface".Let's face it, none of us like gangsters in real-life, but if we decide to watch a film about them, it's not to follow the slice of life of their victims, but just to 'enjoy' the gangster's lifestyle before reality shows us how nasty and ugly it is. That's what cinema is about: a form of escapism. And gangster movies allow us to explore the darkest sides of humanity: the easy way, temptations, showing how crime does pay for a little. Crime reflects the very idea of "The World is Yours" that drove Tony Camonte's ambition, as if it was better to possess the world for a night than being a schmuck for a lifetime (paraphrasing a famous quote from "The King of Comedy"). And that's why most gangster films follow the pattern set by the early 30's classics: the alluring rise and the disillusioning fall, champagne, cocktails, parties, ladies, some killings in- between, but ultimately, an obligatory comeuppance, and you wouldn't believe how this 'obligatory' adjective is hateful for me. Indeed, no matter how ground-breaking the early gangster classics were, they had to justify that they were not intended to glorify crime but to depict a sad reality that was poisoning society: the birth of organized crime, and its torrents of blood, shootouts, and unfortunate collateral damages. In case the viewers let themselves hypnotized by Robinson, Cagney or Paul Muni's charisma, they had to endure the good old moralizing cops reminding us that a kid died during a shootout, and each shooting is followed by a random woman's shrieking scream. But here is the perversity of the gangster films; people did love Cagney, Muni and Robinson, and I suspect they probably loved Muni, more than anyone, for he gave the most electrifying performance of the three, acting-wise. Yet it was almost ruined by a zealous attempt to obey the Code.In "The Public Enemy", it was the big brother staring at Tom Powers, in "Scarface" but the character was getting more interesting at the end. In fact, "Scarface" suffered the most from the Code, with the police officer hatefully looking at Camonte and holding his suspenders to look tougher. And I'm not comparing the apples of the 30's with the orange of the 70's, you could tell the genre evolved with "Angels with Dirty Faces" when the idea of setting an example was a plot device, not an indirect reference to the viewers, and that worked perfectly, creating the idea of a gangster with redeeming qualities.Which leads me to what I consider the film's biggest flaws, let's say I close my eyes on some embarrassing bits of acting, the mustache cop, the supposedly comic relief "Dope" who wasn't even funny by 30's standards, all the Trinity gangster films had their share of bad acting that fortunately didn't affect the main protagonists. BUT that can't distract from the biggest flaw, which is the characterization: Muni gave a performance that was equal to Pacino, and even at some parts, slightly more memorable. Both characters were embracing the madness of their excessive dreams, money, power, the blonde woman, stuff you can't get by 'saying please'. Now, would you really believe a man like Camonta would crawl like a rat begging for his life at the end? He lost his sister, his best friend, everybody, what meaning could life has anyway at this point. I was glad he tried to escape, at least it meant he was bluffing but it's a sort of childish way to teach us that 'bad is weak' when it's not the case. And don't get me started on Cesca, the sister who quickly forgave the killing of Rinaldo, totally off character, and only died when she realized how a coward he was, what a waste of acting talent. As much as I admire "Scarface", I thought the crawling like a rat was atrociously flirting with moralist propaganda. And that's why I firmly believe that the real maturation of the gangster genre was achieved with the underrated masterpiece "Angels with Dirty Faces".The merit of both "Scarface" was to show the flamboyant appeal of the gangster world and the way an extreme ambition could progressively devour and destroy the soul of its protagonist. Imperial in their rises and Shakespearian in their falls, the two Tony are still ruthless killers, but not without redeeming qualities, it's even ironical that their soft spot cause their demises. And if the remake is criticized for being over the top because it's exactly what the crime world is about, an excess to another, it's about a thug, how he succeeds and how he ends, it's the perfect, the consummate gangster movie, 7 years before "Goodfellas" would be praised for the same reasons. If the film is violent, graphic, and full of profanity but it's as far as the original went, by today's standards, without the corny and moralizing intelligence-insulting ending.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>ChristophCinema</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Say "hello" to my 30s friend</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Whenever someone brings up the movie "Scarface", right away they think of the 1983 Al Pacino version. That movie has become so iconic, and so famous in pop culture with its insane violence and "Say hello to my little friend" line, that people often don't realize that it's a remake of a 1932 gangster movie. It's a shame to, because Howard Hawks' "Scarface" may be the greatest gangster film of the 1930s. Howard Hawks is one of the greatest writers in film history. He had such range, that he could write for various genres and come out so original. He's written sci-fi classics like "The Thing from Another World" (another great movie that gets overshadowed by its remake), comedies like "His Girl Friday" and "Bringing Up Baby", and westerns like "Rio Bravo (which I think is John Wayne's best film). You could just give him a genre, and he would never disappoint. "Scarface" (1932) is definitely one of those classic movies that pushed the boundaries in movie violence. It is pretty tame by today's standards, but if it wasn't for this film, the 80s version wouldn't get to be as violent. This is one of those instances where you can like the 30s version and the 80s version equally. They're both very similar, but also very different. It's actually really cool watching the 80s one, and seeing which parts are original, and which parts were lifted straight from the 30s one. The two films definitely have two different messages they're trying to convey. The 80s version is a dark, bloody version of living the "American dream", whereas the 30s version is about being a rebel and upsetting the establishment. But both films are connected by one thing; the characters hunger for respect and power. Many scenes are pretty graphic for 1932; such as the scene where Paul Muni is going on a cop shooting spree. At first, the film kind of shows how fun it is to be a rebel (doing whatever you want, and getting respect for it), which could maybe influence people to do what Muni does in the film; but of course the film ends showing the price these people pay for choosing a life of crime. The 80s version is good, but the original (I think) is a lot better (and a lot shorter by an hour and 20 minutes). It just broke more ground, and represents the time it was made better. It may not have a "Say hello to my little friend", but this movie doesn't need a single quote to make it classic.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Howard Hawks' 1932 classic gangster masterpiece was a remarkable film during the 30's - during an era where powerful mob figures had major control of American cities, notably our setting for this film, Chicago. But being a 50+ year difference in releases, the 1983 version just adds elements that far surpass the limitations Hawks and filmmakers alike were dealing with in the 30s. Muni's depiction of Tony is very well done and enticing to watch, but Pacino's portrayal of Tony (Montana, in the '83 remake) is a legendary performance that has been praised, mocked and parodied ever since its release.Nonetheless, this film is a classic, brilliant gangster flick that I encourage everyone to see.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>What are you going to do about it?...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This was the message aimed at the authorities when Scarface was first released. Even though it has gone down in history as one of the best films of the gangster genre, it was and forever will be a morality tale incorporated into an amoral gangster world where greed, backstabbing and murder is common place. The morality tale within Scarface works alongside the blatant attempts through dialogue to desecrate the gangsters not only in the narrative but in then contemporary America.The amoral protagonist Tony Camonte (Paul Muni) is introduced to us at his most sinister. His silhouette making its way towards its target whilst whistling a tone portrays Tony as a cold hearted figure, his silhouette expressing a vision of terror and mystery. When his face is revealed to us, the cross - shaped scar on his cheek indicates he is the personification of death and danger within the seedy world he inhabits. It is only the beginning of our trip through this world.Yet this trip does not come without its blatant warnings that go unheeded. Thinking he has the upper hand over the police Tony is warned, "You think you'll get there with a gun but you won't, know why? You've got $1,000 bills pasted across your eyes. Some day you'll fall into the gutter". Although we as the audience understand this warning about the greedy lifestyle Tony leads, it is oblivious to him. Instead he lets his desires take control which results in gang conflicts across town, going head to head with his boss Johnny (Osgood Perkins), muscling his way to the affections of Poppy (Karen Morley) and being linked to multiple deaths. Tony's actions throughout Scarface highlights the theme of morality as he has such disregard for it. He just wants to benefit himself as he stated, "I'm gonna write my name all over this town in big letters". The theme is further shown in the conflict between himself and his younger sister Cesca (Ann Dvorak), who is going through life in similar circles to Tony. "Never mind what I do. It's alright", he tells Cesca once again showing disregard for his own actions.Meanwhile the sub plot is the authorities continuing their attempts to punish Tony for his crimes. This results in scenes where the film blatantly conveys their hatred for gangsters. There is a speech by the Chief (Edwin Maxwell) who criticises people for thinking "these bid hoodlums are some sort of demigods". This leads on to a scene of a panel who state their outrage against the strength the gangsters have over law and order. Again these scenes where attempts by the film makers to get the authorities to act tough on gang crime which was running rampant at the time. Eventually as a result of his actions Tony gets his comeuppance which serves not only as a great finale but also as the final statement by the film makers of the gangster lifestyle.Definite till the end, Tony fails to hold off the police resulting in personal tragedy and defeat. Shown cowering down the stairs, asking the police to give him a break before ending his life riddled with bullets in the street. It hits home the grim realities of his lifestyle and shows the irony that the world he craved for so much was never really his. It serves as the final product of what the film makers thought must be done at the time, to serve justice with force.Overall Scarface works perfectly as a morality tale along with its use of motifs. Particularly the use of the symbol X, an auteur touch to forebode death. Paul Muni was well casted as Tony, a character full of arrogance, selfishness, stubbornness and violence. His character had all the right elements to be conveyed as a man you shouldn't respect and nowhere near the romanticised image some people may have thought gangsters to be.</fullReview>
	</review>

There were, however, some unique and useful shots in the movie. One in particular that I really liked was a closeup of a calendar overlapped by a machine gun. As the gun blasts its rounds, the calendar pages fly off the wall. I thought this was a really smooth way of jumping ahead in time while easily maintaining the film's tone and themes. My main qualm with Scarface is that the message was too direct; the film didn't make you think at all. Obviously, Scarface is an anti-violence and anti-gang film. The film makers really do a great job of throwing that in the audience's face. A good example of this occurs during a scene which depicts a newspaper editor talking to several people, including police. The editor exclaims something along the lines of "How can you just sit back and watch this happen?" as he looks at someone positioned behind the camera, giving the impression that he is talking directly to the audience. All in all, Scarface is a good story told by good actors, and is worth watching when you don't want to think about anything.</fullReview>
