		<date>2012-02-05</date>
		<username>Steffi_P</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>"Slap it out of him"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The gangster movies of the early 1930s were one of the definitive aspects of the talkie era. Of all the movies made back then (and Hollywood was incredibly prolific for those few years) it's the gangster movies that tend to be remembered the most. There's something in the smart dialogue and easygoing violence that seems to have made a hit with cineastes down the years.While most of those gangster movies were fast-paced and punchy, under the smooth direction of Howard Hawks, Scarface is a languid and quietly menacing affair. Hawks was one of the best architects of tracking shots Hollywood has ever known. His tracking shots weren't obtrusive, so you don't notice the cinematic artifice going on, as Hawks subtly changes the landscape before the camera without changing the shot. There's a great example in the opening scene. We home in on the bunch of mafia guys in the aftermath of a party, festooned with ribbons and suchlike, then follow the boss over to the phone, with an ominous empty corridor dominating half of the screen, then delve into the corridor amid the shadows and overhanging houseplants. In this deceptively simple manoeuvre the image has been smoothly transformed without the jerkiness of a cut.Paul Muni was a brilliant actor in a steady dramatic role, but put him in the stylised world of gangland and he founders badly, descending into an embarrassing bundle of Italian American clichs. James Cagney and Edward G. Robinson, while certainly overstated in manner, seemed to slide naturally into their gangster roles. Muni on the other hand looks like he is constantly thinking how to act like a hood. Even George Raft, seen here in one of his earliest roles, though a terrible actor could do a convincing gangster strut if nothing else. Speaking of Raft, you do see here what a disappointingly wooden fella he was, limited strictly to mean posturing. And whose bright idea was it to cast Boris Karloff as a gangster, hmm? But despite these flaws, Scarface's reputation endures thanks to its striking imagery and snappy dialogue. And really, the early gangster movie is probably the one genre that is the most similar today as it was back then. When Scarface was remade in 1983, sure, it turned Tony Camonte into a Cuban and replaced bootleg liquor with cocaine, but the principles of iconic words and iconic killing remains the same. Perhaps the biggest difference is that, though the 1932 movie opens with a token title about condemning organised crime, it actually made the life look enticingly cool. Today's mob movies need pay no such lip service to morality, carrying the gloss of glorification, and yet deep down they genuinely show gangsterism to be depressingly morbid.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2011-12-16</date>
		<username>James Hitchcock</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>The Locus Classicus of American Gangsterdom</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The American film industry has long had a love-affair with organised crime. Like most love-affairs this one has had its ups and downs; it probably reached its nadir in the sixties, when relatively few gangster films were made, but was rekindled by the huge commercial and critical success of the first two "Godfather" films in the early seventies and has continued, on and off, ever since. The roots of this affair probably lie in the fact that the Prohibition years (1920-33), which gave such a boost to organised crime, coincided with the early years of Hollywood. Had the Eighteenth Amendment and the Volstead Act never been passed, this affair might never have been so passionate; in Britain and other countries which never had Prohibition the gangster movie has never attained the same cultural importance as in America, even though the British film industry has occasionally produced excellent examples such as "Brighton Rock" or "Get Carter"."Scarface" from 1932 is one of the best-known of the early gangster films, coming out a year before the end of Prohibition. It is loosely based upon the life of Al Capone, whose nickname was "Scarface" and who is here renamed Tony Camonte. Several other notorious Chicago gangsters appear in fictionalised form; Big Jim Colosimo becomes "Big Louis Costillo", Johnny Torrio "Johnny Lovo", Dion O'Banion "O'Hara" and Bugs Moran "Gaffney". The film tells of Camonte's rise to power in the Chicago underworld. When his original boss Costillo is killed, he transfers his loyalties to Lovo, and it is primarily the ruthlessness of his trusted lieutenant Camonte which enables Lovo to take control of the South Side. (In the film, as in real life, control of the illicit liquor trade in Chicago is divided between the Italian-American gangs of the South Side and the Irish-Americans who run the North Side). Lovo, however, is unwilling to confront the Irish gangs, and this leads to a feud between him and Camonte, who has ambitions to become the undisputed crime boss of the city. There is also a sub-plot about Camonte's sister Cesca and her romance with his friend and hit-man Guino Rinaldo.The film was made before the introduction of the Production Code in 1934; after the Code came into force a film of this nature could not have been made unless the amount of violence was very much reduced. And there is plenty of violence on display here. Like the real-life O'Banion, O'Hara is killed in his florist's shop, and there is an obvious reference to the Valentine's Day Massacre when Camonte orders the killing of several of Gaffney's men. Another detail which would not have pleased the post-1934 Hays Office was the suspiciously close- verging on the incestuous- relationship between Cesca and Camonte, who is pathologically jealous of her affair with Rinaldo.Even before the Code, there were limits to what the film censors would allow. These limits varied from state to state, the New York censors being notoriously strict- but there was a general rule that films should not glorify crime. Ostensibly, "Scarface" fulfils this requirement. It was also known as "The Shame of a Nation", and contains a preface which states that its purpose was to shame the Hoover administration into taking more resolute action against organised crime. Where the film departs from Capone's life story, it generally does so in order to show Camonte in a worse light than his real-life counterpart. In 1932 Capone was serving a prison sentence for tax evasion, the most serious crime which the authorities had been able to pin on him. This was felt to be inadequate retribution for Camonte's crimes, and he dies a coward's death after being shot by the police. (An alternative ending has him being hanged for murder). Camonte ends up killing his mentor Lovo, whereas Capone's mentor Torrio was still alive at the time the film was made.And yet, even though the film observes the letter of the censors' demands, it blithely ignores their spirit. (Perhaps unsurprisingly given that the producer was Howard Hughes, well known for his battles against censorship). "Scarface" does, of course, glorify crime, and this, as much as the amount of violence, might have made it unacceptable in the Hollywood of a slightly later date. (After 1934 director Howard Hawks was to make relatively few crime films- the famous noir "The Big Sleep" being the major exception- but was to make a number of war films and Westerns, genres in which the censors took a rather more relaxed attitude to killings).Paul Muni's Camonte may not be a morally admirable figure, but he is an enormously charismatic one when compared with, say, Osgood Perkins's snivelling, weaselly Lovo. He is one of the cinema's earliest heroic, larger-than-life gangster bosses, the direct ancestor of Don Vito Corleone. "Scarface" is perhaps the film which did the most to establish the popular reputation of 1920s Chicago as (in the words of film historian Thomas Patrick Doherty) "the locus classicus of American gangsterdom, a cityscape where bullet-proof roadsters with tommy-gun-toting hoodlums on running boards careened around State Street spraying fusillades of slugs into flower shop windows and mowing down the competition in blood-spattered garages". 7/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2011-09-21</date>
		<username>JRamos3</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Timeless tale with a call to action</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Firstly, Muni's portrayal of Tony is superb. Muni, with his acting chops fully on display in this film, is able to ensnare the audience with his seemingly maniacal behavior and at times, funny antics. That being said, this movie raises several interesting questions about life in America during the tumultuous period of prohibition. The first of which is, if this movie was meant to be a call to action so to speak against the violence and tragedy that often goes hand in hand with the mobster lifestyle, then why make it in the first place? It seems almost contradictory that in one scene the police are bemoaning the glorification that these murderous bootleggers receive in the press, but in several other scenes, the film is intentionally funny. Don't get me wrong, the jokes in the film, while few and far between, do go a long way to lightening the spirit of an otherwise dark and sordid tale. It just seems to me, given the opening inter titles that make references to the fact that average citizens must rise up against this sort of radical tyranny of mob rule, the filmmakers would have made it their paramount duty not to include anything that would make the characters likable. While admittedly this would have made for a much less interesting piece of cinema, my opinion remains the same.Secondly,as someone who grew up with Brian DePalma's version of Scarface, it was interesting to see the similarities and differences between the two tales. While DePalma's more contemporary tale of immigration woes and living the American dream is obviously identifiable to many people, I believe that this original version of the film is much better suited to leave a lasting image. While the violence is clearly more understated in the this earlier version of the film, it is this same lack of violence that allows the viewer time to truly think and reflect on what he or she is seeing, as opposed to the bloody spectacular that is arguably Pacino's best work. Lastly, but certainly not least, is the way Muni's character Tony kills Guino simply because he was engaging in a relationship with Tony's sister. This heinous act speaks volumes to the mindset and mentality of someone who lives that sort of lifestyle, and that for me, was a truly spectacular part of the film. It is subtle and almost demure, a quick few shots and Guino is on the floor. However, this scene is arguably the most important in the film because it showcases Tony's complete and utter disregard for human life, or any life for that matter. That, to me, is the endearing message of this film: That no matter what, if you cross one of those people, they can and will kill you without hesitation. If the filmmakers had tried and succeeded at making that fact the "moral" of the film, I think it would have been better. Overall though, the film was superbly shot and scripted, with the hour and half(give or take) run time seeming much shorter. I would highly recommend this film to anyone interested in taking a peek into what life was like back in those days.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>"The World is Yours"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>While Scarface had some good acting and fast paced moments, I found it lacking an interesting plot line, character development, and a protagonist I actually cared about. The plot seemed overly simplistic. It followed the exploits of a ruthless, vicious, gangster, Tony Camonte, as he tried to take over in Prohibition era Chicago.I found Tony Camonte's character to be uninteresting. While Paul Muni portrayed him perfectly, the character seemed one-dimensional and lacking in any qualities that would make me care about him or what happened to him. I did begin to think briefly toward the end that Tony might have some depth as a person when he seemed upset about having killed his sister's new husband. This ended quickly when he allowed his sister to join him in the shoot out with the cops and then reacted to her death by caring only that her death left him all alone, rather than being horrified that he'd caused the unnecessary death of his 18 year old sister.Finally, I had to wonder about the purpose of the movie. It starts with a disclaimer about it being an indictment of crime and calling for reform and ends with a neon sign proclaiming that "The world is yours". But between the beginning and the end, there's non-stop gang violence. I wondered if it was a movie shot to entertain or to advocate social reform. Maybe this was a just a reflection of the times in which the movie was made, but I found it odd and distracting.</fullReview>

		<date>2011-09-02</date>
		<username>jc-osms</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Scar tissue</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It might creak with age a little with some rolling-eyes acting and and all but this early Howard Hawks gangster movie still packs a punch and I for one much prefer it to De Palma's trigger-happy gorefest from the 80's. Its historical proximity to the events depicted, right down to the recreation of the St Valentine's Day Massacre and barely concealed depiction of then crime-lord Al Capone in Paul Muni's title character give a real frisson to the piece and took a lot of guts - get a load of that crusading legend before the first reel. Hawks hadn't quite mastered pacing at this stage in his directorial evolution with some turgid scenes holding up the action but considering it was only a few short years since the birth of the talkies, allowances have to be made. Famously, Muni's Tony Camonte character's crossed scarface motif is everywhere the violence is, a brilliantly original device for the time. The actual violence is rarely "in your face" direct, no doubt a sop to the censor of the day, but this only serves to encourage and indeed stimulate Hawks' creativity in depicting the actual death scenes. The acting, as indicated, is mixed in quality, but there's no denying Muni's domination of the picture in the rampaging central role. It's a gift for any actor and he grabs it with both hands. George Raft looks tiny but is effective as Guino, his right hand man who makes the foolish mistaken of falling for his boss's sister while the two women in the lead parts, Karen Morley as anyone's moll Poppy and Anne Dvorak as his rebellious sister both act with spirit. Boris Karloff however demonstrates clearly why he soon after moved into the field of wordless monsters with a supremely stilted performance as a rival hood. There's some pretty low comedy however surrounding the dumb secretary Camonte employs and a weak running gag about his telephonist skills right up to his last breath. I thought there was better acting among the police than the villains in general, Muni and Raft apart. I'm surprised Hawks himself didn't remake this himself as he did a number of his other hit movies but this is still a great piece of cinematic entertainment which at the same time dares to hold up a mirror to society.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2009-11-11</date>
		<username>tieman64</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Get Rich Or Die Rapping</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What's amazing about the original "Scarface" is not how dated and quaint it is, but how little modern gangster movies have broken away from its mould. Aside from attitudes toward sex, swearing and violence relaxing, there's nothing thematically to separate, say, "Goodfellas" or "New Jack City", from Hawks' film.What's most interesting, though, is the way De Palma's remake has so ingrained itself with hip-hop culture. Part of the appeal of De Palma's "Scarface" was the way it shamelessly played games with materialism. You may wear expensive shoes, clothes and designer labels, but who you are at your core never changes. You may look like a million dollars, but you're still dirt from the streets, some impossible to lose "it" always defining you as forever lacking class.The irony is that current hip-hop and gangster rap artists actually celebrate this. "You can take the thug out of the socio-economic context, but you can't take the socio-economic context out of the f**king thug!", they proclaim, proudly displaying their coarse edges and ghetto defined presence like peacocks on cocaine. Rather than hide their roots, they broadcast it with Tony Montana like shamelessness. "You don't like who I am? F**K YOU! MY BALLS TOO BIG TOO CARE!" Like De Palma's film, Hip Hop/gangster rap found a way to transcend its contradictions by ignoring them with "Scarface" like brazenness. These artists throw huge wads of money at the screen, surrounding themselves with hordes of half naked women and the most expensive cars. Commentary on the ghetto, racism and economic impotence gives way to a crude mimicking of white society, Donald Trump kitsch meets the Hugh Hefner playboy image meets hardcore porn meets Lehman Brothers corruption meets video game hyper-reality. "You don't like it? F**K YOU! I BURY THOSE COCKROACHES!"Imagine an MTV parody: "I'm Tony Montana and DIS IZ MAH CRIB!". It sounds odd, but that's how these guys behave, inviting the camera into their massive homes, displaying their money, their toys, their gold teeth, before reminding us that they came from the "streets". They got dead cousins, arrest warrants and bullet holes in their ass. "POLICE CAN'T CATCH ME. I TONY MONTANA! LOOK AT DA PELICAN FLY!"They started with 50 cents but now they got prestige in the form of car rims, gold chains and expensive boats. You don't like it? F*CK YOU! YOU WANNA GO TO WAR? I TAKE YOU TO WAR!Hip Hop and gangster rap had become a vessel for arrogant opulence, a way for people who had grown up with nothing to show off their hard earned wares. Influenced by cinema's glossy gangsters, they turned themselves into walking billboards for expensive European clothing companies and broadcasted their tacky class at every turn. The funny thing is, most of these rappers from the "bling era" wound up bankrupt and broke, forgetting to pay mortgages, taxes and incurring unbelievable debts. Say hello to my little eviction notice.7.5/10 ? An influential film, though it hasn't aged as well as Hawks' "The Big Sleep".</fullReview>
	</review>

If you happened to see the movie in Chicago (your grandparents, rather) you'd wonder if you're looking at a screen or just a very large window. The use of lighting is also striking. 		<shortDescription>Points for inspiration are points well needed.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The 1932 version of Scarface is a forgotten film. Movie buffs will be quick to give it credit for being the best pre-code Hollywood gangster film and while that can be argued, it doesn't hold up as well for modern audiences. Dialog is muffled, pacing is slow and character development is non-existent. When today's viewer is likely to have seen 1983's remake along with 'The Godfather' and 'Goodfellas' it's difficult to give this original as much credit as it likely deserves. Overall my impression of Scarface, the original, is that it's an interesting tale that helped created a genre that is as popular today as it was then. Repeated views will likely be needed on my end to hone in my true feelings because on multiple occasions I found myself being unsure of what was being said and only determined it through context or a few key words here and there that were more clearly spoken. The violence did not phase me, but I appreciated the subtle nature of some of the scenes, allowing my imagination to fill in the blanks. Any fan of the remake and/or the genre should take the time to watch this early 30's gangster film. As a stand alone story it may not be all that impressive, but when you take the time to consider the culture in which it was created and the issues that were important it's impressive that it was able to get made at all. And without it, we may not have some of the masterpieces that clearly followed in its footsteps.Read my complete review (w/ possible spoilers) at CD1083.com</fullReview>

		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Over The Top</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>No need to recap the plot. Of the three pre-Code gangster classics, this one is the weakest. That's mainly because Muni can't summon the piercing menace of a Cagney or a Robinson in the central role, (Public Enemy {1931}; Little Caesar {1930}, respectively. And that's the case despite Muni's heavy- handed bluster. It's like the actor is afraid he can't compete with all the Tommy gun splatter unless he amps up behavior to fuse-blowing level. Unfortunately, his gang leader Tony is over the top without even a hint of needed nuance. At the same time, much of the film's pervasive violence approaches the cartoonish, especially the drive-by's that splatter everything but people. And catch the splatter between the side-by-side cars that incredibly miss even the driver. Now, I'm willing to suspend disbelief in the interests of movie-making, but not when my nose is rubbed in it. To me, these two prominent features undercut the film's status, classic or no. There are positives, however. Dvorak (Cesca) delivers an ace performance as Tony's (Muni) beleaguered sister. Her range of nuance, unlike Muni, is impressive. Plus I certainly didn't see Cesca's sudden turnaround coming. That turnaround suggests a character complexity that deepens the story, but is too tricky even for a 1933 movie to explore. Then too, the not-so- subtle theme of incestuous attraction is a daring one and remains perhaps the film's most distinguishing feature. Raft too registers as the quietly sinister Rinaldo. Too bad he doesn't get more screen time since he could furnish the menace so lacking in Tony. Frankly, I didn't spot any of director Hawks' trademark stylings. Likely, it's still too early in his career, but he does keep things moving.Anyway, except for the incest angle, the plot's become a pretty familiar one of a gangster's rise and fall. I know my general opinion is a minority one, particularly about Muni's turn, but I don't want to risk conflating loud talk with good acting, regardless of reputation.</fullReview>
	</review>

This brought a real sullen feeling to the shots and contributed greatly to the overall scene. One memorable scene in the movie was the scene where there are 7 men lined up against the wall and all the camera shows is their shadows. The men are executed by a firing squad, bullets spray onto the wall, and the men drop to the ground. This extremely graphic scene made it so people could get a close look at how horrible the things happening were during this time. Very exciting and well-made film overall.</fullReview>

		<username>Ashley Lingenfelter (a-lingenfelter@hvcc.edu)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Visually Stunning</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Howard Hawks's "Scarface"is easily the most entertaining studio era film that I've ever seen. Paul Muni's performance is equal parts chilling and intoxicating, and definitely lent to the believability of the whole film. The visuals were also essential in creating the suspenseful atmosphere of the film; particularly concerning it's use of shadows. Rather than show actual gore and carnage, the film allows for our imagination to run free, as most of the brutal deaths are somehow concealed or suggested by shadows. For instance, in one especially gruesome scene seven men are murdered, and you can see their bodies fall like dominoes, one after the other. The horrifying effect of this scene is just as, if not more effective than it might have been with more obvious gore. I also thought that the camera movements were really interesting, and often strikingly poignant. such was the case in the incredible final scene. The camera drifting from Tony's body, past the mob of screaming cops and civilians, up to the "The World Is Yours" billboard; that's a whole lot of irony for only 15 seconds of film.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>"Get outta my way, Johnny, I'm gonna spit!" "Scarface" is a film that moves like a hurricane: swift, merciless, and brutal. Driven by Paul Muni's maniacal performance and Howard Hawk's keen and concise direction, the film, while often preachy and melodramatic, still holds up today, offering quite a wallop.The film is not subtle in its purpose: to expose gangsters for the dangerously immoral criminals they truly are. I can imagine audiences at the time racing home and locking their doors after having witnessed all the on screen chaos and violence the film unabashedly emphasizes.Of course, the highlight of the film is Muni's madhouse portrayal of a man out of balance, chewing up the scenery and spitting it out. His wild eyes, slimy grin, and mocking one-liners make "Tony" one of the most repulsive, and yet somehow likable characters in film history - perhaps it is because while Tony may be vicious, it is only because he is not very clever, like two fists swinging in every direction. Nonetheless, it is an iconic performance.Overall, while the film does have its flaws and some elements have aged better than others, it is still quite a rousing and jolting piece of old fashioned entertainment - one to please any fan of the shoot'em-up genre.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Scarface</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>THE WORLD IS YOURSThe rise and fall of a cocky, confident Latino hood from poverty to tailored suits and finer living who uses guns and violence to get to the top as the #1 Chicago gangster only to come apart because of his unstable protectiveness over a sister he wants no man to go near. Paul Muni really loses himself into the role of Tony Camonte, only concerned with taking whatever he wants, starting as a "body guard" for one kingpin he shoots (his whistle, a signature before he plans to murder someone, in this scene, along with his shadowy silhouette on the wall, is iconic), joining up with another he considers a bit cowardly because Johnny Lovo (Osgood Perkins) isn't keen on moving in on the North side of Chicago, an area ran by another mobster named Gaffney (Boris Karloff).Booze is the profitable product coveted by gangs who want to monopolize the industry in Chicago. Camonte sees the North side as easy pickens despite Lovo's reservations. When Camonte starts using Lovo's thugs on a violent rampage, shooting anyone who stands in their way, including destroying property (it is well established that children are even caught in the middle, bellies full of hot lead), it isn't long before Gaffney runs out of men to protect him (resulting in a tragic fate inside a bowling alley).Howard Hawks' masterpiece hits the ground running, and has a pace that never lags, with a cast that sinks their teeth into the parts, lots of memorable faces and colorful characters who I think inspired the Dick Tracy comic strip. The noirish look of the movie, how the streets are ominous and imposing, dark and foreboding, gave birth to a whole genre of classic cinema. Muni's dangerous, take-no-prisoners, volatile mobster would be a precursor to James Cagney who took this kind of character and made it his own. I must say that the violence even surprised me, particularly for a 1932 picture, how so many people are laid to waste by machine guns or shot dead in close range. Buildings are riddled with bullets and Muni's scar-faced gangster seems immune to how devastating his actions are to the public at large. The scenes with Muni and Dvorak (what a woman! She just dominates her scenes) are quite eyepopping and less-than-subtle regarding the incestuous feelings Demonte obviously has for sis. Cannot forget George Raft?and his memorable coin-flipping signature?as Demonte's right hand man, Guino Rinaldo, one cool customer with a level-headedness his pal is in short supply of. The movie's message, about controlling the crime populating the streets of the city, is laid on thick, but has relevance, even today.</fullReview>

		<date>2011-05-09</date>
		<username>BenjaminBufordBlue</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Stands out among many pre-code gangster films</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>More violent and raw than any of the crime films of its period, "Scarface" stands out as one of the greatest gangster films of the pre-code era, and of all time. Paul Muni's portrayal of a mean, nasty, and imposing boss is captivating, and Director Howard Hawks not only crosses lines that other gangster films had not crossed, but he includes clever and entertaining symbolism, such as the X to symbolize death. The film had trouble passing the censors as it was seen as glorifying the gangster lifestyle and too violent. Several scenes had to be added or changed to give the film a more condemning feel, including adding the subtitle "The Shame of a Nation." This film with its unapologetic portrayal of the gangster lifestyle, is directly responsible, more so than almost every other film, for the implementation of the Hays Code a couple years after its release.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Paul Muni is "Scarface" in this 1932 film, beautifully directed by Howard Hawks and Robert Rossen, with a screenplay by Ben Hecht. It's precode, so we see Poppy (Karen Morley) shacking up with Johnny Lovo, Tony, and possibly Rinaldo (Raft). We also see Tony's not so subtle incestuous love for his sister (Ann Dvorak). Dvorak looks amazing in the early Joan Crawford hair and makeup - she sparkles in the role of a confused young woman looking for love in all the wrong places, no doubt to get away from Tony.Dark and disturbing, the most striking thing about Scarface is what goes on during the silences. The best scene takes place in Johnny Lovo's office. Tony believes that Johnny set him up to be killed. He escapes and, with Rinaldo, goes to see Johnny. Johnny knows that he knows. The tension with just the silence in the room is unbelievable. Sheer brilliance.Paul Muni was an actor from the Jewish theater. He created his characters from the outside in, and, as was the style of the day, he was given to a certain amount of melodrama in his performances. Here he is a total thug -- and, unlike Cagney and Bogart, very ethnic in appearance, with a clumsy walk and a vanity about his appearance. He's truly excellent and more than up to the final moments of the film."The World is Yours" the Cook's Tour neon sign says -- this is an anti-mob movie urging people to insist that government do something about mobsters and take back the streets. It would be a long time before that happened, if it's happened yet.</fullReview>

		<username>sme_no_densetsu</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>The original gangster classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>While "Scarface" wasn't the first notable gangster film (it was preceded by both "Little Caesar" &amp; "The Public Enemy") it was undoubtedly the most controversial of the pre-code gangster films. Loosely based on the life of Al Capone, "Scarface" doesn't skimp on the violence while unfolding its tale of prohibition era gangsterism.Playing the titular lead role was Paul Muni who contributed a larger than life portrayal full of swaggering machismo. However, his character wasn't just a one-dimensional goon; Muni seized the opportunity to show multiple facets of the man, from ruthless gangster to devoted brother. While the supporting cast doesn't quite rise to the same level as Muni, there is nevertheless notable work here from Karen Morley, Osgood Perkins &amp; Ann Dvorak. You'll also see George Raft &amp; Boris Karloff, though I wouldn't count their performances among the film's best.Howard Hawks' direction captured the action admirably though I'd say that the explosive content overshadows the film's craftsmanship. The screenplay (by the talented Ben Hecht) is daring and features some nifty wordplay from time to time.In the end, though, the inevitable question is how does it compare to Brian de Palma's 1983 remake? Personally, I prefer the later version for its memorable script and fine performances. Some of the earlier film's themes benefit from expansion while the violence was ratcheted up a notch or two. For this reason, I find the original "Scarface" mostly of interest as a pre-code time capsule, albeit one executed with considerable vigour.</fullReview>
	</review>

The final scenes (just like the remake) have Tony held up in his home trying to avoid all the bullets and shooting some of his own, and after Cesca is shot dead, he goes outside and is blown away (the ending back then would have been Tony going to court and hanged). 		<shortDescription>Awesoem Movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I thought that Scarface was an awesome movie. It was a lot better than I was expecting. The protagonist of the film, Tony Comonte, was ruthless character and Paul Muni did a brilliant job portraying him. The character was a really interesting twist between a cold blooded killer and a sort of playful child. He treated the whole gangster life style like a big game. A good example scene is when he gets the machine gun from the rival gangsters. The fully automatic weapon is new to him and he acts all excited and giddy about it; saying stuff like "he Johnny, look what I got!" and "lookit Johnny, you carry it around like a baby." It's a really funny scene, but at the same time a little disturbing. After that scene Tony goes on a rampage, wiping out guy after guy, and you really get a sense of how cold hearted the character is. Especially when he lines up seven or so men against a wall and basically executes them all with the machine gun. Comonte is a strange character beyond just his ruthless blood lust and playful childish attitude. He also has like a weird obsession with his younger sister, and even goes as far as killing his beast friend over it. Which is probably the strongest example of his coldblooded personality. The whole movie is pretty brutal, which surprised me a good deal since I always assume older movies to be real prim and proper. I had never know about these "pre-code" films in the past, but I can easily tell that this film is one of them.The writers really get their point across about the horrific nature of mobs and gangsters. The scene that really drove the message home in my opinion was when the police chief gives a short speech about the whole situation. He talks about stuff like kids getting shot while playing hopscotch, how people have unhealthy romanticized attitudes toward criminals, and about old west criminals and how at least those guys fought each other fairly. It's a great speech, and is probably one of my favorite scenes in the movie.I really liked how shadows were used in the movie as sort of substitutes during violent scenes. You see what's going on, but you don't really see it. The effect does a great job producing a dark, malicious atmosphere. Overall I was really impressed by the film.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>scarface</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The film Scarface from 1932 is one of the original gangster movies. Although it is a movie from over seventy years ago, it has all the qualities a present day gangster movie would have. It has family drama, illegal substances, an over abundance of guns, and of course an underlying love story. The main character Tony, played by Paul Muni, is portrayed as a bad gangster, but also has hints of a likable family man. In the beginning he starts out as an average spot among the gang, but quickly moves himself up the ladder by way of greed. Tony gets a taste of money and power and quickly decides that's the life which he chooses to lead. As a travels down this path he is quickly blinded by once motivated him. He decides that the woman he wants is one which is with his friend. One of the first bad decisions that Tony makes. This is a line that once crossed can never be gone back on. Tony also has a younger sister Francesca, which has also gotten a taste of the same lifestyle. Throughout the entire movie, Tony as a man changes changes to a point of no return. His family thinks that he is nothing but trouble, his friends are beginning not to trust him, and there's nothing that he can do about wither situation. Everything in Tony's life comes to the boiling point when he goes to visit Francesca, and one of his gang members open her apartment door to greet him; Tony breaks. He thinks that he is protecting her when in fact he is breaking her heart. This movie is very well written in the sense that it shows the effects that power and money can have on a man. You may start out as a descent family man, but once you get a taste your whole world can change. By the time you realize what has become of your life, it is usually too late to go back and change what you have become.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Scarface review</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I've just finished watching the classic movie Scarface, a gangster life propaganda film starring Paul Muni. This movie was entertaining in many ways. The cinematography was interesting and well thought out. During the opening scene, the camera pans around a lonely street lamp flickering against a fading night sky into early morning. There has been a party all night thrown by a notorious gangster named Big Louie. In the same scene, however, Louie is approached menacingly by a shadow creeping into his home, intending to slaughter him (no doubt an attack of gang relation) This camera effect of following Paul Muni's character as a shadow is menacing, and thats just what his character is. This film, like many others of its era, has long scenes without breaks (it is 5 minutes into the film before the second scene even begins) During gun wars between North and South side are long, drawn out, loud, and shot from very unique angles. The audio, even for an older film, is sharp and clear. Even when focusing on characters with heavy accents, it isn't hard to hear the dialog. This movie is extremely well put edited. As for the acting in this film, as I've stated before, Paul Muni is incredible. He is so well animated and his facial expressions are over the top. In the scene where he meets his love interest Poppy played by actress Karen Morley he imitates her plucking her eyebrows- it's hysterical. His thick Italian accent accompanied with such facial expressions makes his character so believable. I really enjoyed this movie! After seeing this version, I appreciate it so much more than the re-make.</fullReview>

		<username>planktonrules</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Pretty good film, but 1931 seems to have been a bit better vintage for Warner Brothers gangster films</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie is a thinly veiled biography of Al Capone. Some names and incidents were changed, but many were left intact (such as the nickname "Scarface" and the St. Valentine's Day Massacre). The biggest difference was the ending. In the film, Scarface died in an expectedly melodramatic way, whereas in real life, Capone slowly wasted away due to the ravages of syphilis.For the most part, the film is what you'd expect from a film about gangsters, but it was a little more crude in its execution. In contrast, Warner's other two big gangster hits from the year before, LITTLE CAESAR and PUBLIC ENEMY, seemed more polished and with better, less shrill acting (particularly Muni's and Dvorak's). I definitely prefer these other two films.About the only really unusual and unexpected thing about the film was its strongly implied incestuous relationship between Scarface and his trampy sister. While they never exactly said they slept together, their relationship was very, very unnatural and the audience was left to wonder and draw their own sordid conclusions! The addition of this plot element and liberal sprinkling of violence and sexual innuendo isn't all that surprising, really, as Howard Hughes produced this film and these are pretty much the norms for his films.</fullReview>
	</review>

I found myself counting down the minutes until it was over and I could say I gave it a chance. To sum up, 'Scarface' to me was boring, unimaginative, insufferable, and disappointing.</fullReview>

		<username>earlytalkie</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Top-Notch Gangster Thriller Still Packs Punch</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a rarity, a classic old film from the early talkie days which I have never seen, prior to tonight. Howard Hughes did things in a big way when he made films. His Hell's Angels of 1930 was a top-notch aerial epic. Scarface, made in late 1930, but unreleased until 1932 is a top-notch gangster picture. Beautifully directed by Howard Hawks, the film's dark and brooding cinematography glistens. The performances by all concerned are first-rate. Paul Muni was truly a great actor, and he infuses his character with life. Karen Morley is very good as the girlfriend, but Ann Dvorak is simply great as the sister who is looked after just a little too carefully by Muni's Tony. There are many killings throughout the picture's 94 minute running time, but people looking for blood-and-guts had better head for the Brian DePalma 1983 remake. The violence, and the tension leading up to some of the killings are there, all right, but things were a bit more subtle in the early '30s than they were 50 years later. There is a symbolic "X" theme which runs through the picture which is quite interesting and something I've not seen too often. Boris Karloff has a small part in the proceedings, and while this film was released in April, 1932, which was after Karloff's star-making turn in Frankenstein, Scarface was filmed and completed before the famous Universal horror flick. Scarface is a satisfying gangster picture which is rightfully regarded as one of the very best in it's genre, regardless of year. The DVD print is very good, and there is an alternate ending included which lends a more properly moral tone. I liked the original ending as seen in the released film better, but you can judge for yourself when you see it. This is a film which is well worth your time.</fullReview>
	</review>

	<review>
		<date>2011-05-15</date>
		<username>tomgillespie2002</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Thrilling and violent Pre-Code gangster film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Commissioned by Howard Hughes in the Pre-Code era, Scarface arrived in a time when the public had a growing interest in organised crime. The James Cagney films The Public Enemy and Little Caesar both had a nasty gangster as their protagonist, and portrayed their rise and fall through the criminal underworld. Whether the audience had a genuine fascination with a very real problem at the time, or they just enjoyed the exploitative violence and colourful characters, I don't know. Scarface remains one of the best examples of the genre, and a film that would directly influence many a modern-day gangster film.Tony Camonte (Paul Muni) is the chief lieutenant in the new criminal organisation overlooked by Johnny Lovo (Osgood Perkins). They plan to bootleg alcohol on a large scale throughout the city, and Johnny hopes to unite all the gangs so they can operate efficiently without any trouble. Tony is warned not to try to muscle into the turf ran by an Irish gang, but Tony ignores these orders, and begins an extremely long and violent war. With his coin-flipping second-in-command Guino (George Raft), Tony gains more power, and soon the greed and ambition take over him. He also has an over-protective, almost incestuous, relationship with his sister Francesca (Ann Dvorak) and frightens off any man that shows an interest in her. Little does he know that she is secretly seeing Guino.After many demands for script re-writes by the Hays Office (who would later introduce the ridiculous Hays Code), Howard Hughes finally put the film in production and told director Howard Hawks to 'make it as realistic and as grisly as possible'. Only passed by the censors a year after production was finished, Hawks would have to change the ending and introduce a title card damning the actions of the kind of criminals that the film portrayed. Still, Howard Hughes was forced to release it in states that lacked censorship rules, after refusing to edit the film any further.The film still remains extremely violent even by today's standards. On one hand, it's an interesting commentary on the corruption of the American Dream, showing how greed can consume, and on the other it's an enjoyable and rough gangster film with exciting and impressively realised gun-fights and drive-by's. It's all anchored by Paul Muni, who gives a convincing and nasty performance. His character acts completely without remorse, and therefore extremely unlikeable. Tony Camonte is obviously based on Al Capone, complete with identical facial scar (hence the title), and apparently the set was visited by Capone's thugs.I was quite surprised how much the Brian De Palma/Al Pacino remake took from this - the sibling jealousy, the shootout ending, the double- crossing of his boss - and out of the two, I feel this is ultimately the better film. There's just something authentic about the film that the remake failed to capture.I'll certainly be tracking down some more Pre-Code crime films after this, and if any of the others can capture the excitement and the grit of this, then it will be a great experience. And watch out for a small roll for Boris Karloff!www.the-wrath-of-blog.blogspot.com</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2011-02-08</date>
		<username>Seamus Dowling</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>A film without an ounce of subtlety or realism.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Scarface(1932) falls victim to its filmmakers mixed motivations. On one hand the film's stated goal was to reveal the scourge of organized crime to the American audience and encourage further government action against gangsters. The other goal was to produce an exciting and entertaining film that would be commercially successful. In this reviewer's opinion the film totally fails in both respects.First, the film fails in its goal to bring the harsh realities of organized crime to the audience as there is nothing about the film that feels real or genuine. Aside from a strong performance by George Raft as Little Boy, there isn't a single believable character in the film, only caricatures. Paul Muni's Tony seemed to drift in and out of a bizarre Italian accent throughout the film. Clearly the character was meant to be a psychopath, perhaps his inability to decide if he had an accent or not was a symptom of his psychosis? The sniveling and weaselly mob boss Lovo had absolutely no credibility making it impossible to believe that any mobster would respect or fear him enough to work under him for any period of time. Ann Dvorak's Cesca character has emotions and motives that seem to have little basis in the real world, we could see the trouble was going to cause coming from a mile away. Vince Barnett's Angelo was also unbelievable but at least the character was at times genuinely funny. But, again, the lack of subtlety hurts the comedy as the audience is hammered over the head with Angelo's antics. The very strong, unnatural looking lighting style of the films also adds to the unnatural feeling of the film and the cartoon violence of indiscriminate gun fire and one-punch knockouts cement the fact that no one could mistake this film for real life.The film also failed in being a pure entertainment piece as the judgmental tone and the sense of Tony's impending comeuppance weighed heavily throughout the film. The through-line of the story was so clear and the ending was so telegraphed that I found it impossible to get lost in the enjoyment of the action and the occasional comedy.If Scarface had attempted to just be a cautionary tale about the dangers of organized crime or just a cartoonishly violent and over-the-top depiction of mobster life it may well have worked. But because it attempted to be both, it totally failed.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-02-25</date>
		<username>datboiprich05 (datboiprich05@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Scarface</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The film I am commenting on is the original Scarface. I picked this film because I am a big Tony Montana and Scarface fan. So once I seen that the first Scarface film was originated in 1932, I had to watch it so that I could analyze it. As I was watching it I was looking for different shots to dissect so I could break down this film. So within this critique I am going to describe the different shots that were used to make this film.The Mise en scene of the film was very basic. The scenes within the film were very basic. They consist of a simple backgrounds, very little effects, fading in and out of each scenes, and the actors and actress within the film could have used a little more work at doing their job.The angles in the film were also very basic. In one particular scene, Tony and his boss received their weapons (Tommy guns, Pistols, and many of bullets) in a warehouse full of other individuals. The angle that was used was just a straight forward angle that could only focus on the both of them. There were individuals moving in and out of the warehouse and also crossing their face. According to my knowledge I believe the shot that was used for this scene was the medium close up shot. Even though Tony and his boss were the two main characters in this film, I believe that the main point of this scene was to get everybody involved in the scene but they either did not have the knowledge to do so or they did not have the proper cinema equipment to make it happen. Because of the medium close of shot, the duration of the scene was not very good. Yes indeed this was a great movie to watch. This scene could have used some extra work. This particular scene was about the same length as the rest of the scenes in the movie, but there was not ever a continuous set of still images. So with that being said, the camera did not move throughout the entire scene. Even though there were people moving in the background and closely to them Tony and his boss, the camera did not focus on any particular individual entering or leaving the warehouse. The other characters who actually had parts within this scene had to approach Tony and his boss directly instead the camera adjusting to where they were at already. I think they should have used the Extreme long shot because this could have made their movie even better. In that case, their audience would have had a better picture about what was actually going on in this particular scene.There is another scene within this film that I am going to comment on. This scene is when Tony meets his bosses wife at a diner to have lunch. As they sat down to eat their lunch, a group of individuals performed a drive-by shooting at the diner that Tony and his bosses wife was eating at. The camera moved very little within this scene even though there was a lot that took place in this particular scene. When the camera, it moved side to side (laterally) so they could get the full body of Tony and his bosses wife as they sat down at their diner table that they were going to sit at. The motion that was used was called the "pan" motion. They should have used this motion throughout this entire scene so they could have made this scene better. The drive-by shootings would have been a great opportunity for them to use the "pan" motion's shot. Instead of using the "pan" motions shot, they used the eye-level shot. By using the eye-level shot they could not film the vehicles as they continued down the street as they passed the cameras on the street they were being filmed on. Also within this scene, Tony's assistant was using the diner's telephone. Even though the camera motion was not up to par in this scene, I believe that the duration of this scene was pretty good. Because of the events that took place in this scene, I think they were forced to focus more on a particular shot because of their lack of knowledge to maneuver their camera around to fit everything in one whole shot. First was Tony and his bosses girlfriend that they focused on as they were being shot at. After them was Tony's assistant. Tony's assistant was using the diner's telephone during the time of the drive-by shooting. As he was associating on the phone they showed bullets hitting objects around him but they did not show where they were coming from. So with that being said, instead of the film director actually filming the individuals doing the shootings and the individuals being shot at, he just showed they individual that was being shot at.Overall this was a pretty good movie. Even though this film had flaws that needs to be fixed, it was still a great movie. I would not have ever noticed any of the mistakes in this film if I had not been taught the proper knowledge of how to brake down and describe different shots within a film.</fullReview>
	</review>
